## 636

B”H, 5 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

...At a time of favor, I will mention you at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in accordance with the content of your letter. It is surely unnecessary to point out that the more one adds in matters of Torah and mitzvos—those involved in this matter—the more Hashem Yisbarach adds in His blessings for what one needs.

Although it is not comfortable to write the following, I find it my duty not to avoid it: many times, such cases with children are the result of the parents not having been properly observant, at the time (i.e., when the mother conceived the child), of the laws and statutes of Taharas HaMishpacha. If, in this case, this was also so, it is self-understood that when one wishes to rectify what has occurred, one must first rectify that which caused such an occurrence. Although, on the face of it, this is a matter of the past, as is well known, the instruction of our holy Torah is that "nothing stands before teshuvah." A solution has been provided for this as well: the teshuvah must be greater. This means that there must be a double effort: that they themselves (the parents) should **strictly** observe the laws and statutes of Taharas HaMishpacha in the future, and that they should also influence others, as much as is within their ability, to spread the matters of Taharas HaMishpacha. Then, in the manner of the well-known language of the Midrash, "You have saved a soul, etc.," Hashem Yisbarach will see that through them many children have been saved from this matter (through the observance of Taharas HaMishpacha by their parents), and this will bring a remedy, with all its results, to their own child. May Hashem Yisbarach help you find the appropriate words, with "words that come from the heart," in a way that will bring it into action.

With blessings for good news.

P.S.

On this occasion, I will remind you regarding the observance of the three daily portions—Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya—which are well known, as we heard many times from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that they are essential for every soul and are a *segulah* for many matters.

## 637

B"H, 6 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn

To the Brownsville-East New York
Ladies Auxiliary for Yeshivas
Tomchei Tmimim Lubavitch,
May Hashem grant them life

Blessings and peace!

It was with pleasure that I received your notification regarding the annual banquet, which is to take place this week, Wednesday, Parshas Beshalach.

This is the week of Shabbos Shirah and the day of the *shirah* (song), for the *shirah* is found in the fourth *aliyah*, which belongs to Wednesday—*revi’i b’shabbos*.

The conclusion of the *shirah*—and everything follows the conclusion—is the song of Miriam the Prophetess. Without the song of Miriam, the song of Moshe Rabbeinu would not have been complete. The song of Miriam **with joy** and **all the women** completes the *Az Yashir* of Moshe and the Bnei Yisrael.

With this, the holy Torah provides an instruction for Jewish women of all generations: that the Jewish woman has a great responsibility and merit in the holiest moments and most important events in the history of our people.

Krias Yam Suf was a preparation for Kabbalas HaTorah and for the Divine revelation at Mount Sinai. The Jewish women had an important part in that preparation.

Today, too, we stand on the path toward Divine revelation, through Moshiach Tzidkeinu speedily in our days. The preparation for this is through Torah and mitzvos and the *pnimiyus haTorah* in the illumination of Toras HaChassidus. For this purpose, Yeshivas Tomchei Tmimim Lubavitch was founded, and was transplanted to America by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, whose *yom hillula* also falls out this week.

I hope that the above will give each one of you the necessary encouragement to increase your work on behalf of the Yeshiva with *mesiras nefesh* and *simcha*. At the same time, may you strengthen all matters of Torah and mitzvos with Chassidic warmth and light, each one in her own home and in the surrounding environment, through which you will draw down Divine blessings to be successful also in your personal matters, both materially and spiritually.

With blessings for good tidings in all that was mentioned above.

## 638

B"H, 7 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Shabbos Kodesh, together with the *pidyon nefesh* that will be read at an auspicious time at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, may his merit protect us.

In it, you ask how one can be saved from evil thoughts.

The matter is already addressed at the very beginning and inception of all four parts of the *Shulchan Aruch*: "I have set Hashem before me always" is a great principle of the Torah, etc. Immediately, fear will come to him, etc. See there in the *Tur* and the *Shulchan Aruch*, at the beginning of *Siman* 1, in the *Rema* and in the *Shulchan Aruch* of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe.

To strengthen the effect of this contemplation, it is appropriate that you study until you know by heart the beginning of Chapter 41 of the holy *Tanya*, up to page 56b, the words "before the King."

And since for everything one needs *siyata d'shmaya*, it is appropriate that every weekday, before the morning *tefillah*, you set aside a few coins for *tzedakah*, and that you keep the three known *shiurim* of *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*.

With blessings.

## 639

B"H, 7 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

We acknowledge receipt of your letter from Erev Shabbos Kodesh, along with the *pidyon nefesh* which will be read at a time of favor at the holy resting place of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

In it, you ask for the advised counsel on how to fulfill the mitzvah of "Love your fellow as yourself."

It is surprising that such a question is asked, given that this is an explicit commandment in the Torah. Furthermore, Rabbi Akiva stated that it is a great principle of the Torah. To add intellectual reasons to the matter, you should study with concentration the *maamar* in Chapter 32 of the *Tanya Kadisha*, as well as the section on the mitzvah of *ahavas Yisrael* in the *Sefer Taamei HaMitzvos* by the Tzemach Tzedek. You will see there wondrous things regarding the extent to which this matter reaches.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 640

B.H., 7 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 27 Teves, in which you ask for assistance in maintaining consistency in your Torah study, as well as how to deal with those who mock you, etc.

The answer is already stated at the very beginning of the four sections of the Shulchan Aruch: "I have set Hashem before me always" is a great principle in the Torah, etc. Through this, one immediately attains yirah, etc., and will not feel embarrassed, etc. See there in the Tur and the Shulchan Aruch at the beginning of Section 1, in the Rema, and in the Shulchan Aruch of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe.

To strengthen the practice of this hisbonenus, it is appropriate that you learn by heart the beginning of Chapter 41 of the holy Tanya, up to page 56b, the words "before the King."

2) Regarding the way to influence your friends:

The general point here is as the Sages of blessed memory stated: "Words that come from the heart enter the heart." The heart is called the "king" of the body, through which one influences all the limbs, which are the vessels for action, speech, and even thought. It is understood that the more you increase your own conduct to be in accordance with the instructions of our holy Torah, the more you will succeed in influencing your friends. For there is no greater mystery than a living example in the very matters one wishes to influence in one's environment. In general, our Sages have informed us: "Any person who has yiras shamayim, his words are heard" (Berachos 6b).

Your pidyon nefesh will be read at an auspicious time at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

Surely you are aware of the three daily study cycles—Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya—and you keep them. We have heard many times from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that they are suitable for every soul and are a segulah for many matters.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

There is room to say that this might even be the primary factor, especially in this *dara d'ikvasa d'Meshicha*, similar to what is brought in the *Midrash Rabbah* that for the giving of the Torah, there needed to be a complete number of six hundred thousand, etc.

## 642

B"H, 8 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the G-d-fearing man, who occupies himself with the needs of the community, etc.,
R' Ephraim Eliezer, may he live, HaKohen

Peace and blessing!

...To point out the connection and the order—from above to below—of the eve of Shabbos, the Shabbos that passes over it, the *milah*, and the *parsha* of the day (the sixth [day]—as publicized through the enactment of my father-in-law, the Rebbe), the *parsha* of the *Man*, which is the one that proves (and is pushed aside by) the holiness of Shabbos (see *Likutei Torah*, *siddur ha-maamarim* "LaMenatzeach Al HaSheminis," regarding the connection to Yosef). The *milah* is a "small matronita," and the "great matronita" relies upon it. And the *milah* is beautiful (*Nedarim* 31b, "from the implication of the negative..."), and the daughters of Israel are beautiful, for a woman is considered as one who is already circumcised.

## 643

B"H, 8 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, who engages in communal needs, a branch of a noble tree,
R' Schneur Zalman, may he live and be well,

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter of Monday, along with the *pidyon nefesh* enclosed therein, which will be read at an auspicious time at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory—

In it, you share the good news regarding the founding of the holy institution, established upon the purity of holiness, at the Beis Medrash Adas Yisrael. My letter to those involved has been sent, and I will certainly provide further explanation should it be needed.

The beginning is rooted in the end: our Rebbes, the *Nesi'im*, invested immense efforts in founding the first *yeshiva*, beginning with our great Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe, and with even greater intensity and strength—despite the concealments and obstructions—in the founding of Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim. These powers are active in our generation, a "generation of orphans," to increase Torah and make it glorious within circles that are constantly expanding and spreading. To use the precise language: the dissemination of the *maayanos* **outward** (*chutzah*) specifically. Through this, we draw closer to the fulfillment of the well-known concept, "Wisdom (*chochmos*) cries out in the streets" (*chutz*). As explained at length in Chassidic teachings (in *Toras Chayim*, at the beginning), *chochmos* is plural, referring to both the revealed and hidden aspects of Torah, which cry out with joy and gladness specifically in the "outside." And this is well understood.

It is a well-known saying of the Baal Shem Tov that a soul descends below and is clothed in a body for seventy or eighty years for the sake of doing a favor for a fellow Jew, whether in material or spiritual matters. How much more so is this true regarding a lofty matter such as the education of children in the purity of holiness—which is an eternal matter, for those being educated will build children for eternity, the seed blessed by Hashem, for generations and generations until the end of the world.

May it be the will of Hashem that for long, good years, and with peace of mind and proper health, you will continue to share good news regarding your holy work in the aforementioned area. And in the measure of the Holy One, Blessed be He—which is "measure for measure," only many times over—may you derive much *nachas* from your children, may they live and be well, both you and your wife, may she live and be well.

With blessings for good tidings,

To prevent future heartache and to rule out the possibility of the "shepherds of Gerar" claiming, "The water is ours" (especially if, over time, some other institution participates in the expenses)—which in any case hinders the conduct of "broadness" (*rechovos*), and on the contrary, see Ramban on that verse—it is worthwhile to establish firm parameters regarding this matter as soon as possible, by setting a name for the institution, and so on. My suggestion is "Ohalei Yosef Yitzchak - Lubavitch," as these branches already exist in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt speedily, in Morocco, Australia, etc. It may also be appropriate to create a charter, which also provides a way to secure (*baavarnen*) the aforementioned. This is especially true since a charter is surely necessary in any case so that donations to the institution can be tax-deductible. Under the aforementioned name, it may be possible to append it to the charter of Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch or Machne Israel, which already possess these rights.

## 644

B.H., 8 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn

...Regarding the yahrtzeit of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, which falls this Friday, Erev Shabbos Kodesh Parshas Shirah, I wish once again to encourage and inspire every one of the Nshei Ubnos Chabad, may they live, and indeed all of Klal Yisrael, to strengthen their work for Yiddishkeit in general, and specifically according to the program and within the framework of your branch. Strengthen it, and also expand it—in accordance with the circumstances of our time, the final hours of the dark and bitter galus, the ikvesa d’meshicha, which demands an expansion and deepening of the work for Yiddishkeit, illuminated and permeated with Chassidic light and warmth.

And as in all important moments of our history, so too now, Jewish women must take an active part in this, according to the ways and paths of our holy Torah.

We learn this lesson from the parsha of the day as well, Parshas Shirah. The conclusion of the shirah—for everything follows the conclusion—is the shirah of Miriam HaNeviah. The shirah of Miriam **with joy** and **all the women** completes the "yashir Moshe u’vnei Yisrael."

With this, the holy Torah provides a directive for Jewish women of **all generations**: that the Jewish woman has a great responsibility and privilege in the holiest moments and most important events in the history of our people.

Krias Yam Suf was a preparation for Kabbalas HaTorah and for the Divine revelation at Mount Sinai. The Jewish women had an important part in that preparation.

Today, too, we stand on the path toward the Divine revelation, through Moshiach Tzidkeinu, speedily in our days. The preparation for this is through Torah and mitzvos in the illumination of Chassidus.

True contemplation of this great privilege and great responsibility will surely give each one of you the necessary encouragement to increase your work with joy and with Chassidic warmth and light—each one in her own home and in her surrounding environment.

And through this, you will also draw down the Divine blessings to be successful in your personal matters...

6-Mem

B.H., 9 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi, man of truth, G-d-fearing, scion of a noble lineage,
R. Yaakov, may he live,

Greetings and blessings!

I received with pleasure, in due time, the Siddur you published, *Siddur Tefillah Tzelusa D’Avraham*, Part I, adorned with notes and illuminations from the crown of sources for prayer and customs.

I found in it a treasure trove of clarifications regarding the text of the prayers and matters related to it. It is well known what our Sages said regarding prayer, that these are "matters that stand at the pinnacle of the world" (*Berachos* 6b), from which the importance of every single detail is understood and is self-evident. This is especially so according to Rashi’s precise commentary on the spot regarding the meaning of "pinnacle of the world"—that it ascends on high. This is also understood from the fact that the earliest authorities were meticulous in all matters of prayer, down to the very word, as they stated that they would weigh and count the number of words in the prayers and blessings (*Tur*, Section 113).

As is also known from our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, author of the *Tanya* and *Shulchan Aruch*, who counted it as a special merit that he clarified and established the text of the prayer in his Siddur. It is told by Chassidim that when he was clarifying the text of the prayer, he had sixty Siddurim before him, from which he compiled it.

The value of citing sources for matters of prayer is even greater in our current era, since the disregard mentioned in *Berachos* (loc. cit.) has only increased in our generation—an orphaned generation. This disregard often manifests to the point where anyone who wishes says whatever they please, doing as they like with the prayer texts, changing, shortening, and skipping, and it goes without saying that they invent new prayers, the likes of which our fathers and forefathers never imagined.

This is the opposite of what we were warned against, forcefully and decisively, in the explicit verse: "Do not be rash with your mouth, and let not your heart be hasty to utter a word before Elokus..." (*Koheles* 5:1). See there the commentary of the Ibn Ezra, who discusses this at length. And the R. Chaim Vital reported in the name of his teacher, the Arizal, regarding even *piyutim* and *pizmonim*, that they only approved those "that were established according to the path of truth, but these later ones, who did not know the path of Kabbalah, do not know what they are saying and err in the order of their speech without any knowledge at all" (*Pri Etz Chaim*, Shaar 1, at the beginning).

Because of the preciousness of the subject and the affection for the matter, I have added a few notes in the margins.

I hope that many will bring your Siddur into their homes and study it, and most importantly, that this too will bring them to the intended goal—to contemplation before prayer, as we are commanded: "When you pray, know before Whom you are standing." And this is an explicit halachic ruling: "One should contemplate before prayer (i.e., at least three times on a weekday, and four times on Shabbos and Yom Tov! — The Author) the greatness of the Almighty, may He be exalted, and the lowliness of man, and remove all human pleasures from his heart" (*Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim*, beginning of Section 98), and the law is based on the Mishnah: "One does not stand to pray unless..."

And then, certainly, at the time of prayer, the prayer itself will be appropriate to its nature, and in the language of Rashi, as mentioned above—it will ascend on high.

With respect and blessing.

***

**Notes**

**At the beginning. 14a. Laws 1. Tallis Katan.** And he recited the blessing on the *tzitzis* before putting it on. Now, it is understood that **halachic rulings** in general, and those in a Siddur in particular, must be the final conclusion, regarding the reality and in practice.

Such a reality, where the law is as stated above, is extremely rare in our time, because the *tallis katan* must have the required measurement, and if not, it is a blessing in vain, with all the severity of that law (this was mentioned in passing—later in your Siddur, but in the part intended for those who study, 24b). And, alas, there are very many whose *tallis katan* does not have the desired measurement, etc. And it has already been ruled, in any case, that one who wears a *tallis gadol* should, at the time of the blessing over it, intend to exempt the *tallis katan*, and one relies on this even if it does have the measurement. From this it is understood that all those who do not have a *tallis katan* of a certain measurement and are not yet wearing a *tallis gadol* should listen to the one blessing over the *tallis gadol*. These matters are explained in the *Acharonim* on *Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim*, 68, 16, 17. And this is not the place to elaborate.

**End of Introduction. 41b.** Four *tefachim*, as it should be. And in the explanation, how much it is forced, etc. — The version "four **tefachim and something**" — this is how it is in the *Siddur HaArizal* of R. Shabsi of Rashkov. And see also *Pri Etz Chaim*, Shaar HaChagim, Ch. 3. And in *Shaar HaKavanos*, the matter of Sukkos, 4d — regarding the *pas* of four *tefachim*.

In passing, regarding what is written there (in the explanation) about what is found in the *Siddur HaArizal* that *or* is a *kezayis*, etc. — see *Shaar HaKolel* (on the Siddur — by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, R. Levi Yitzchak) Shaar 48, Section 2 (and there he also brought from the *Responsa Hashiv Moshe*. And it is in *Choshen Mishpat* 16, a response to the *Chasam Sofer*).

**Siddur, at the beginning. 1.** The precision that you returned in me "my **neshamah**" — corresponds to the version of the blessing "Who restores **neshamos**." And in this blessing, one can say the reason according to the *peshat* — that we mention and awaken the act of Creation: "And He breathed into his nostrils the breath of life."

Regarding what you noted in the language of the verse "Into Your hand I entrust my **ruach**" — see on this *Shaar HaKavanos*, *Drushei HaLaylah*, 10d: And the reason for saying "I entrust my *ruach*" and not "my *nefesh*" or "my *neshamah*," etc.

**Siddur, at the end. 448, Rosh Chodesh.** An exception regarding fasting — one must add 1 Tishrei — Rosh Hashanah (and note from *Tzafnas Paneach* — by the Gaon of Rogatchov — *Mahadura Tinyana*, p. 142b).

**Ibid.** The prohibition of work on Rosh Chodesh, and it is brought from the testament of R. Yehudah HeChassid not to get a haircut or cut nails. — It depends on clarifying the reason of R. Yehudah HeChassid for this, whether it is because of a prohibition or because of danger (in which case it is not a matter of the prohibition of work) — and this has already been noted in the commentary *Meshek Eliezer* — and as we find those who say that after midday one should not get a haircut, etc. (*Siddur* and *Shulchan Aruch HaArizal*, *Pri Etz Chaim*).

## 648

B"H, 12 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished head of the community, [name], may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

I received your letter in due time, bearing the good news of the farbrengen held in the shul and beis medrash where you serve as one of the leaders. It was a celebration of the bright day of 19 Kislev, the Rosh Hashanah for the study of Chassidus and the ways of Chassidus. It is well known that the precision of the term "head" (Rosh)—and not "beginning" (techilah)—implies that the head encompasses and draws vitality into all the limbs, [representing] the days of the year.

May it be the will of Hashem that throughout all the days of the coming year, for good and for blessing, there be an increase in the power of vitality and light in all matters of the shul and beis medrash, so that it may be fully consistent with its name: a Chabad shul.

The merit of all those who exert themselves and are involved, standing at the helm, is explained in many places. It is an explicit verse (Daniel 12:3): "And those who justify the many shall shine like the stars, forever and ever." It is my hope that each person will strive to also fulfill the beginning of the verse, "And the wise (maskilim) shall shine..." through an increase in the study of the teachings of Chabad, which is the true wisdom (haskalah). And surely, the promise of the verse will be fulfilled, that they will "shine like the radiance of the firmament." The Tikkunei Zohar (at the beginning) explains that the "radiance of the firmament" includes all colors... from this radiance, many souls are illuminated... and these are the wise (maskilim) who possess the intellect (sechel) to understand the secrets of their Master.

Since everything stated in our Torah is surely an instruction, it is within the ability of each one of them to achieve this; the matter depends only on their will. Furthermore, when they exercise their will, they are assisted from Above with the greatest possible assistance, for the merit of the many assists them.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 649

B"H, 13 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, and distinguished, etc.,
R' Menachem Nachum, may he live,

Shalom u'Bracha!

Through the esteemed Rabbi, the Chassid, G-d-fearing, and distinguished, who engages in communal needs and accomplishes much, R' Shmuel Dovid HaLevi Raitshik, the shaliach, a pidyon nefesh was delivered to me with a request for a blessing for himself and for his son, Shabsi, may he live.

At an auspicious time, I will mention them at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for whatever they require, in accordance with the content of the pidyon nefesh.

It is surely unnecessary to remind you that the more one adds in matters of Torah and mitzvos, the more the Giver of the Torah and the Mitzvos—who leads the world and oversees each and every person with Divine Providence—adds His blessings for what a person and his household require.

The essential point in this is the matter of "Love your fellow as yourself," which Rabbi Akiva called a great principle of the Torah. This is also part of the content of the weekly parsha, Parshas Yisro, and the Giving of the Torah. As explained in the holy Zohar, when was the Torah given? Only after Yisro also acknowledged, saying, "Now I know that Hashem is greater than all the gods"—the advantage of light coming from within darkness. Likewise, in every time and era, there must be a "Giving of the Torah" for every Jew; "they shall be new in your eyes." As the phrasing of the blessing states, "Who gives the Torah" (in the present tense), there must be the matter of "extracting the precious from the vile"—whether in others, or even within oneself, in the subtle matters of iskafya, each according to his standing and situation. See also Tanya, Chapter 27 (page 34b), "And they would starve themselves for two hours for this intention, etc."

You are surely aware of the three daily study portions—Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya—which we have heard many times from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, are appropriate for every soul and are a segulah for many matters. In any case, you should observe them from now on.

With respect and blessing for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

#### ***649***

**R' Menachem Nachum:** Feldman, Omaha.

## 650

B"H, 13 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed and distinguished Rabbi Nassan Dovid, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from Sunday.

At a time of favor, I will mention you and your wife, may she live, at the holy gravesite of the Baal HaHillula, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, whose Hillula we have just observed, in accordance with your request.

Regarding what you noted in Tikkunei Zohar, Tikkun 69, near the beginning (99b): "And the man knew, etc. There it says, 'I have acquired a man [ish],'" and you raise the difficulty that in the Torah it states that Chavah said this.

The Gra emended the text to read, "She said, 'I have acquired a man,'" but this emendation is problematic, as the entire continuation in Tikkunei Zohar there speaks about **Adam**, as it says, "And the man knew; he knew his duty, etc.; he knew the filth, etc.; he saw the reincarnation, etc.," all of which is in the masculine form. It is understood that it is a great difficulty to change all of the above to the feminine form.

To my mind, this can be resolved otherwise: even though initially "she said" (Chavah), "I have acquired a man," this name was **established** [by Adam]. Certainly, a name cannot be established without Adam HaRishon agreeing to the name, as he is the primary one. Therefore, the Tikkunei Zohar explains why he agreed to it.

It is also worth noting the commentary of the Daas Zakenim Baalei HaTosafos on Bereishis 38:5, which I heard from my teacher: that this was the custom—he would name the first son, and she would name the second son (although the Ramban disagrees with this).

## 651

B"H, 14 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

And this is well understood.

Surely, you are adding to them through your methods of influence—I refer to [the influence of] depth—and similar to what was said above regarding the "outside" in different people, this also applies to the "outside" within these very people, time after time. ## 652

B"H, 14 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn

Blessings and Peace!

In response to your letter—which did not indicate the date of writing—in which you write about your brother, may he live, who in his opinion is now at a crossroads, having finished his army service and subsequently a mechanics course, yet he has not decided how to settle down, and he is twenty-two years old.

My opinion is clear, based on what I have seen among the youth in several countries, and I have also examined this regarding the youth in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established: that a period of several years at your brother’s age is, in the vast majority of cases, preparation for the entire duration of life that follows. That is to say, any effort during these years to establish a worldview and a perspective on life is worthwhile, and one should not be stingy with time for this, because this investment pays off many times over—that is, throughout the decades of life that follow. Furthermore, it is certainly unnecessary to elaborate that our era, which is full of events and confusion, necessitates a more established and firm worldview, especially since without this, there can be no peace of mind or harmony in the soul, which are the essential conditions for a well-ordered life.

Based on the above, the conclusion regarding your brother, may he live, is understood: he should dedicate at least half a year or a year to the study of our holy Torah, which is called *Toras Chaim* (the Torah of Life). This title is not to be taken figuratively; it is a guide for our lives in this world, including the day-to-day life of the weekdays, and not only for Shabbos, Yomim Tovim, and the Yamim Nora'im. As mentioned, this is not a waste of time; on the contrary, it is a strengthening for the decades that follow. After your brother, may he live, passes through this period of fortification, he will decide what is appropriate for his perspective at that time regarding how to settle down, in terms of the manner and the location.

We see tangibly that the most effective propaganda is that which comes not only through speech but through action—that is, a living example of the matter and the ideal for which one is advocating. Certainly, in this case as well, if in your own conduct and entire way of life you are a living example of the conduct of a *bas Yisrael*—each of whom is called a daughter of Sarah, Rivkah, Rachel, and Leah—this will certainly influence, to a great and significant extent, the conduct of your family members, your brother included.

Finally, I must respond to the beginning of your letter, where you mention that you are not a "Chabadnik," yet you write to me. This contradicts the saying of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe, author of the *Tanya* and founder of the Chabad Chassidic system, who said that this system is the inheritance of the entire Jewish people, every man and woman among them. The system and its content are not to blame for the fact that there are circles that have not yet accepted it in its entirety. Especially regarding a significant portion of these circles, they are not to blame for this, but rather secondary and external causes. It is understood that they do not have the ability to diminish their connection to the system, as this is merely a lack of knowledge, which will surely be corrected in time.

With blessings for good news regarding all the above, and for success in your influence on your surroundings in accordance with what has been stated above.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 653

B"H, 14 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the Avreich, Aharon, may he live,

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from the day of the Hilula, together with the *pidyon nefesh* that will be read at an auspicious time at the holy gravesite of the *baal hahilula*, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

In it, you write regarding the communal activity which, in your words, is "new of its kind," referring to activity that also involves girls.

But in truth, this is an ancient matter, as was also discussed at the *farbrengen* this past Motzaei Shabbos regarding the verse, "And Miriam answered them" (*Vata'an lahem Miriam*). The word *lahem* (to them) is in the masculine form (rather than the feminine form, which seemingly should have been used according to grammar). In *Tanchuma*, it states that when the Jewish people ascended from the sea, the Jewish people and the ministering angels came to recite song. The Holy One, Blessed be He, said to the ministering angels, "Leave the Jewish people first..." Afterward, the women and the ministering angels stood [debating] who should praise first. Rabbi Chiya said that the angels [should go] first and then the women (and this is why *lahem* is in the masculine form—referring to the angels). But Levi said, "By the heavens (an oath), I will not accept this; rather, the women praised first." And even according to the first opinion, the ministering angels were required to receive permission from the women. It is well-known what my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, the *baal hahilula*, said (on the Seventh Day of Pesach, twenty years ago) regarding the superiority of the women with their drums and dances over the song of Moshe and the Jewish people; see there.

May it be the will [of Hashem] that this activity, too, will be met with extraordinary success. Included in this, first and foremost, is increasing the number of participants—both those who are influenced and those who are the influencers—and the rest is understood. Happy is the portion of those who involve themselves in this.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 654

654

B”H, 15 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing and learned, occupied with the needs of the community,
Rabbi Yisrael Chaim, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter from the eve of the New Year for Trees, with the enclosures of the *kabbalos* (resolutions) included therein.

It is surprising that you do not mention the name of the man who, according to your letter, is G-d-fearing and trembling [before Hashem], yet poor in funds. His intention is certainly desirable, and it is my hope that you will find illuminating words to explain to the aforementioned the teaching and story of our Sages (*Vayikra Rabbah* 3:5) regarding the matter of "the offering of a poor man comes first." It is well known what is explained in *Chassidus* based on the statement of the *Zohar* (Vol. III, 195a) regarding a similar matter concerning *tefillah*, which takes the place of *korbanos*: that there is a "prayer of Moshe," a "prayer of a poor man," and a "prayer of David," and the "prayer of a poor man" is the one that comes first.

On this occasion, you should also explain to him the well-known *shiurim* of *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*. May it be the will [of Hashem] that these words emerge from the heart and enter the heart.

With blessings for good tidings, including regarding his personal situation.

**Rabbi Yisrael Chaim:** Friedman, Brooklyn.

## 656

656

B"H, 16 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, the G-d-fearing man, who occupies himself with communal needs,
R' Nissan, may he live and be well,

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter from Rosh Hashanah La'ilanot, along with the enclosure.

At an auspicious time, I will mention your wife, the Rabbanit, may she live and be well, as well as yourself, at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, regarding the matters you require, in accordance with the content of your letter.

We channel merit to a meritorious day, for "man is a tree of the field," and Rosh Hashanah for the tree draws down vitality into all matters of the coming year. As is known, the precision of the terminology "Rosh" (head) and not "techilah" (beginning)—as explained by the Alter Rebbe and his son, the Mitteler Rebbe—is that there should be a drawing down of an increase in the blessings of Hashem for what each person requires, in all their details.

Especially for those who disseminate and exert themselves in the matters of the "Tree of Life," where there are no undesirable matters, as in the well-known statement of the Ra'aya Mehemna explained in Iggeret HaKodesh, Epistle 26. May it be His will that for long, good days and years, you will increase strength for Torah in general, and for the teachings of Chassidus and its matters in particular. For this is "my vineyard" in a unique sense, in addition to the portion in the vineyards in general—which are also vineyards according to Torah, yet they are not "mine." It could be said that this falls under the category of the revealed law: a mitzvah that cannot be performed by others, which is at the highest level, taking precedence over Torah study, even though "matters of Heaven," i.e., a mitzvah in general, cannot be compared to it even in one detail, etc. (Moed Katan 9b). I am certain that you have also utilized the third volume of his book, regarding which you write at the end of your letter about its imminent publication, especially since it is dedicated to the days when everyone agrees that a Jewish person must occupy himself with matters of fear of Heaven.

With respect and blessing for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 657

B"H, 16 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter in which you outline what you have experienced over the past few years, and where you express your certainty that I will not reproach you, because—in the words of your letter—you "cannot" help [yourself], you "cannot" [do otherwise] regarding the study of Torah, regarding the avodah of tefillah, regarding engaging in chinuch al taharas hakodesh, and so on.

It appears that you do not suspect yourself at all that there might be even a remote possibility that the situation is not that you "cannot," but rather that you "do not want to." And regarding the conclusion of your letter, where you write that you are like other "simple people": while it is understood that every person must strive to increase and enhance their own virtues, to exempt yourself from all these obligations—which stem from the heritage of your fathers, through the chinuch you received for many years and the environment in which you were situated—and to exempt yourself from all of this by declaring, "I am just a simple person," is something that clearly has no basis.

As for your question regarding whether to act in a certain direction concerning your future arrangements, it is also simple: every one of the Bnei Yisrael must strive for an arrangement that allows him to fulfill his role as stated in the Mishnah, "I was created to serve my Maker." The greater the possibilities—even if those possibilities came without toil or effort, but rather through inheritance or the chinuch of one's parents, as mentioned above—the greater the responsibility and the role, and along with it, the greater the merit and the assistance from Above.

May it be Hashem’s will that you recognize the truth of the Chazal adage that "a person is close to himself," and therefore he does not judge his own situation truthfully, sometimes leaning to the right and sometimes to the left. May you act according to the instruction of your mashpia in the study of Chassidus, and may you perform [your avodah] in a way that is truly personal (*aliba d'nafshai*). May you share good news regarding all that has been mentioned.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 658

B"H, 17 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from Rosh Hashanah La’Ilanos, in which you write the content of your conversation with Dr. [Name] regarding the matter of separation [between boys and girls]. Although you do not write the points that Mr. [Name] conveyed in my name, he surely also conveyed that this is not exclusively a matter of the Jewish religion—though that in itself is sufficient. For even the nations of the world have, in recent years, begun to see the damage caused by co-education, as is known to educators regarding the alarming situation in many schools in this regard. However, for reasons that are understandable—though refuted—they suppress the reports on this.

It seems I also mentioned in my conversation with Mr. [Name] that since, ultimately, one must first and foremost rectify the situation in practice, there is also another possible approach. I refer to increasing the number of students in the classes in such a way that they will, in any case, require a second teacher or at least a teacher’s aide. There is room to say that even if they feel that an effort is being made toward separation, they will overlook the underlying reason because of the aforementioned [practical necessity], as this will provide a face-saving way to retreat, and the rest is understood.

It was pleasing for me to read at the end of your letter about your speech before the congregation regarding the *baal hahilula*, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukulleh"h, nevg"m, zya*, Nasi of the Jewish people. May it be His will that his merit protect each and every one of us, speedily and in our days, in fulfilling the task of every member of the Jewish people, as the Mishnah states: "I was created to serve my Maker," in a manner of inner vitality and goodness of heart, as guided and demanded by the *baal hahilula*.

With respect and blessings for good tidings.

## 659

B”H, 17 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the avreich, Yosef, may he live,
Shalom u’Bracha!

In response to your letter of 2 Shevat, in which you write that there is a method of teaching halacha (Kitzur Shulchan Aruch) and Gemara only to those students who commit to eating kosher, presumably with the intention of thereby forcing at least some of them to eat kosher:

Nevertheless, I am not comfortable with what you have stated. For the study of Gemara, and certainly the study of halacha in its own right, is an end in itself and a great mitzvah. In our current era, it is only within the walls of an educational institution that there is a suitable opportunity to instill in the student knowledge of Torah and mitzvos (unlike the previous era, where such knowledge was absorbed at home and even outside). From this it is understood that any deficiency in this regard during the years the student is in the educational institution is a distortion that, it is fair to say, may be impossible to rectify.

Another point supporting my position is that the very study of halacha and Gemara prepares the student and draws him closer to the performance of mitzvos, even if he himself is not yet connected to them. This is based on the Mishnah, “A mitzvah leads to another mitzvah,” and the statement of our Sages, “The light within it [the Torah] returns him to the good.” It is also understood naturally, for when one studies a subject time and again, one thinks about it more frequently and more deeply. Furthermore, there is another reason for what has been said: it is not within a person’s authority to withhold the study of Torah from school-aged children, and even from adults, and this is well-understood.

Regarding what you wrote about your nusach of davening, it is understood that you should adhere to Nusach Sefard of the Ari z”l, as is brought in the responsa of the Acharonim, that one should change from Nusach Ashkenaz to Sefard, but not the reverse, and the reasons are explained there.

May it be Hashem’s will that you increase in Torah and mitzvos, permeated with the light and teachings of Chassidus, and that you be one who is "moist enough to moisten others" among those you influence. And there is nothing that can stand before the will.

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 60

B”H, 19 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn

To the students of the Mechina of the holy Yeshiva Toras Emes
in the Holy City of Jerusalem, may it be rebuilt and established,
May Hashem keep them alive.

Peace and blessing!

With pleasure I received your letter from the eve of the bright day, the day of the Hillula of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. In it, you write of your resolution to foster true love and brotherhood among yourselves, to observe the Yeshiva’s order both in quantity and quality, and to speak with one another from time to time regarding strengthening your learning and your conduct in the ways of Chassidus and Chassidim.

May it be the will [of Hashem] that the merit of the Baal Hillula, who sacrificed his holy life for the dissemination of Yiddishkeit in general, and Chassidus and its matters in particular, stand by you so that your resolution will be enduring for long days and years. [May it be] in the manner commanded in our Torah, the Torah of truth, that one should "increase and go forward," "increasing in light." For it is said, "A truthful lip shall be established forever," and all the more so regarding the attribute of truth, and even more so regarding the "truth of truth," as explained in Chassidus regarding the three distinctions of "a truthful lip," "truth," and "truth of truth." And although it is incumbent upon you to do all that depends on you in all that was mentioned—as it is written, "And you shall choose life"—this *is'arusa d'lesata* (awakening from below) brings about an *is'arusa d'le'eila* (awakening from above) in an incomparable manner, as understood from our Sages’ statement: "Open for Me [an opening] like the eye of a needle, and I will open for you [an opening] like the entrance of a hall."

May it be the will [of Hashem] that from the Mechina you will ascend in due time from level to level, and serve as a living example of the conduct of a student in an institution whose foundation is that of our Rebbes, the Nesi'im, of blessed memory, and under their guidance. I look forward to hearing good news regarding your progress and your advancement from strength to strength in all that was mentioned.

With blessings for success in Torah and fear of Heaven.

## 661

B"H, 19 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

Following the longest of interruptions, your letter of 8 Shevat was received... You ask how to influence him and the family members in general to bring them closer to Torah and mitzvos.

The guidance of our Torah, the Torah of life, regarding this is well-known: the path is as it is written, "Its ways are ways of pleasantness, and all its paths are peace." Even though there is no room for compromise in matters of Yiddishkeit, in order to successfully influence others to draw near to it, one succeeds to a much greater degree and in a shorter amount of time through the aforementioned paths of "ways of pleasantness" and "paths of peace." This is also evident in tangible experience. Furthermore, this ensures that one has sufficient patience to speak once and again, and not to become angry, chas v'shalom, if in the initial days one does not yet succeed in the conversation, etc.

## 652

B"H, 19 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and blessings!

...Regarding what you wrote about not seeing your own progress in matters of Chassidus, etc. — this is certainly nothing but a temptation of the *yetzer*, for it is **impossible** that the performance of *mitzvos* would not have its effect, *chas v'shalom*, and especially since it is the "Tree of Life" where there is no [death/diminishment], etc. (**see** *Iggeres HaKodesh*, beginning of section 26).

Since the progress is a reality, it is only that for the time being you do not see it — the essence is there. And surely, in time, the sight of it will come as well. Especially through the war against the *yetzer* in its own matters, which will naturally lead to a weakening of the *yetzer* regarding these temptations and the concealment it creates. However, it is necessary that from time to time you study Chassidus before [teaching/sharing it with others]... and this merit of the many will assist you in further progress regarding yourself. And this is easy to understand. I will await good news.

## 663

[19 Shevat, 5718]

After a long interval, your letter from Rosh Hashanah La’ilanot was received, in which you write that although you have succeeded in establishing two synagogues in the ma’abarah, etc., the situation is difficult, even from a spiritual perspective, compared to their situation before they ascended to our Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established.

It is surely unnecessary to elaborate that if in the Diaspora—the lands of the nations—we are commanded to "ascend in holiness," how much more so in the Land upon which the eyes of Hashem your G-d are from the beginning of the year to the end of the year, the palace of the King, the King of Kings, the Holy One, blessed be He. Not only is there no room for a decline, Heaven forbid, relative to one’s conduct in the lands of the nations, but there must be an increase and elevation compared to the previous situation. And if this is the case even for those who were compelled to ascend to the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, then all the more so for those who had the choice and decided of their own volition to ascend to the Holy Land. This decision requires—and necessitates—a prior resolution to increase in the holiness of the body compared to the lands of the nations; and this is understood.

May it be His will that you find the appropriate and illuminating words to explain the above to all those over whom you have influence and whom you reach, and, of course, in a manner that the matter is brought into action—that is, regarding an increase in daily conduct—and may you bring good tidings regarding all of the above, soon.

#### ***663***

Printed in Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 14, p. 216.

## 604

B"H, 20 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

To the distinguished, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent, engaged in communal affairs,
Rabbi Yitzchak, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 9 Shevat, as well as those preceding and following it, in which you write the main points of your holy work regarding the Reshet, the affairs of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad, etc.

It was a pleasure for me to read, in particular, about the youth gathering in Kfar Chabad, and I am certain that more detailed reports on this will be received.

May it be the will [of Hashem] that the impression of these gatherings (and not just an impression, but tangible actions) will continue throughout the days that follow. As I have written many times, such gatherings—and even more internal farbrengens—are in the category of plowing and sowing. It is self-evident that after this, work is required so that the growth will be as it should be and successful, and this is well understood.

As is evident from your letter, despite all my letters of encouragement and arousal from here, the absolute recognition has not yet penetrated that the affairs of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad are not in the category of an obligation or a burden upon the members of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad, but rather a matter of privilege. Furthermore, it is also the channel and vessel for spiritual sustenance and physical sustenance as well. By "sustenance" here, I mean all of a person’s needs—food, clothing, a home, and children, life, and ample sustenance. Even though, on the face of it, it is strange that this truth is not recognized, when it is already understood simply from many maamarim of Chassidus, sichos, etc., the world says: "One does not ask questions about a fait accompli," especially when the *dorein marko* (the spirit of the times) is in the middle. However, this very knowledge should diminish the *he'elem* and *hester* (concealment and hiding), and on the contrary, turn darkness into light, along the lines of what is explained in Iggeres HaKodesh, Epistle 25, at the end, regarding the interpretation of the verse "that man has dominated man to his detriment." This means that through this very thing, a person is aroused to daven with more *kavanah* from the depths of the heart, etc. The intent of the entire Torah and mitzvos—and even the matters of "in all your ways know Him"—is the matter of *tefillah*, a connection, a joining, and a bond with the Ribono shel Olam. May it be the will [of Hashem] that you find illuminating words to explain this matter to yourself and also to your other colleagues, and in the circles that are constantly expanding, for ultimately, the aforementioned point is a pillar and foundation to lighten the work in the service of Hashem.

Regarding what you wrote about giving *maaser* for *tzedakah*, the question is somewhat surprising, since this matter is in the Shulchan Aruch, and especially within the community of Chassidim. On the contrary, if, as you say, they are not managing with their current salaries, then there is room for the advice of Chazal: "Salt your wealth [by giving to charity] to preserve it," and not the opposite, G-d forbid.

Regarding what you wrote about utilizing the content of the letters from the first night of Chanukah concerning fundraising as well, the doubt is surprising, since it is explicitly discussed there. As for the fear that this will cool down the seriousness of the matter so that they will mobilize for actual instruction—those who wish to utilize this for the aforementioned purpose do not need notification from anyone regarding the content of the letter, since it is a general letter, and anyone interested, for whatever reason, can become aware of it.

Regarding what you wrote about detailed instructions concerning the Tzeirei Agudas Chabad reserve—it is surprising what needs to be more detailed after the **multitude** of letters that have been received. It is true that in these letters it was not specified that so-and-so should deal with this detail and so-and-so with that detail, because this must come through *is'arusa d'lesata* (arousal from below) in order for the engagement to be lasting. As is known, the matter of *kabbalas ol* (accepting the yoke) is a foundation and root, but it is impossible to work for a long time with *kabbalas ol* alone; there must also be a matter of *taanug* (pleasure), satisfaction, etc.

B"H, 20 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

## 657

B”H, 21 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Rosh Hashanah La’ilanot, in which you write:

1) That you are experiencing fluctuations, and even though you resolve otherwise from time to time, you do not maintain your resolution, etc.

The answer to this has already been stated in many places in Chassidus, including in the Tanya, that “one nation shall be stronger than the other,” etc. One must not become discouraged or fall into sadness, G-d forbid, because of this, since for many this is the path of their avodah, as explained at length in many places there (you can find them in the subject index at the end of the Tanya). Any despair regarding spiritual matters—and such despair in particular—is nothing but a temptation and tactic of the yetzer hara, and this is well understood.

You surely know of the three daily shiurim—Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya—and in any event, you should observe them from now on.

## 659

[21 Shevat, 5718]

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from 1 Va’era, along with the enclosure. You have surely received my previous letter by now, in which I believe the style of my response addressed the proposal you made in your last letter.

Regarding what you wrote concerning Krias Shema, specifically the completion of the 248 words, and in particular the conclusion regarding what I wrote about this in Krias Shema al Hamitah—where it seems there was an inaccuracy—I am hastening to correct this based on what I found in my notes, which was also transcribed in *Hayom Yom*, 28 Adar. It states there: "In Krias Shema al Hamitah, at the conclusion of the three paragraphs, one says the word 'Emes.' And there, on 17 Iyar: 'In the Krias Shema that some say in the morning—before davening—in order to fulfill the obligation of Krias Shema at its proper time, one also repeats the three words [Hashem Elokeichem Emes] and concludes with the word 'Emes.' In the Krias Shema of Rabbeinu Tam’s Tefillin and Shema Rabeinu, one does not repeat them, but one does say 'Emes.'"

Regarding what I wrote concerning Krias Shema al Hamitah—that one also says the word "Emes"—this was intended to negate the interpretation held by some regarding the text in the Siddur of the Alter Rebbe for Krias Shema al Hamitah, "Shema Yisrael until the end of all three paragraphs," which some take to exclude the word "Emes." That is not the meaning; rather, the intention is that one should recite all three paragraphs. As for completing the 248 words in Krias Shema al Hamitah, I have not heard this explicitly, but I believe that *anash* follow what is written in *Shaar HaKolel* on the Siddur, Chapter 36, subsection 4—to repeat the words "Ani Hashem Elokeichem."

2) Regarding what you wrote concerning *Yoshev B’Seser* in Krias Shema al Hamitah and the variations in custom therein: it is somewhat surprising that you do not mention that the variation concerns not only the conclusion but also the beginning—as many are of the opinion that one begins with *Vayehi Noam*. Regarding the points you noted:

a) *Sefer Chassidim* holds a unique view on this, as in his opinion the "Song of Plagues" includes—and **begins** with—"Hashem, mah rabu tzarai." This is also found in the *Hagahos Bris Olam* on *Sefer Chassidim*, which is based on the Yerushalmi of Shabbos and the end of Eiruvin.

b) In general, it is puzzling—and I believe it is not found anywhere else—that a title would be applied to a chapter in Tanach when the intention is only for a portion of it and not the whole. As mentioned above, in the case of the "Song of Plagues," the intention is only for a part of the psalm and not the whole. However, in *Ateres Zekeinim* (Siman 295) and in *Tzror HaMor* on Vayikra, it is implied explicitly that the entire psalm is the "Song of Plagues" (and even the verse *Vayehi Noam*?!).

c) As for the reason why all those who say to conclude until *Me’onecha* [do so], it could be said that even in their opinion, the obligation is only until *Machsi*, but because of the rule of our Sages, "Every verse that does not conclude [with a break]..." they finish the verse. We should not create a dispute where none exists.

d) In any case, one must consider the custom on Motzaei Shabbos—according to **all** opinions—to recite the entire psalm of *Yoshev B’Seser* until the end. According to the Zohar (Part I, 14b)—and see *Mateh Moshe*—the reason is that this is the time to recite the "Song of Plagues." Why should it be different from Krias Shema al Hamitah? Although, of course, it can be said that on Motzaei Shabbos there are additional reasons for reciting this psalm, beyond the reason of the Zohar, and because of those reasons, one recites the entire psalm. It is already known what is written in the *Tur*—cited by the *Magen Avraham* (Siman 295) and in *Machatzis HaShekel* there—that the entire psalm, which consists of 136 words, is a *segulah* for blessing, and for this reason one also repeats the verse *Orech Yamim*. However, in that case, one must also include *Vayehi Noam*, as explained there. But the *Ateres Zekeinim* mentioned above attributes it to the "Song of Plagues."

## 670

B"H, 22 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 17 Shevat, in which you write:

a) Regarding your state of health and your personal situation, and you conclude that at times you reach a state of despair, G-d forbid.

It is surely superfluous to elaborate on the fact that the matter of despair is absolutely negated. As our holy Torah, the Torah of life, commands: "A person should never despair." This is explained at length in the books of Chassidus, including the holy Tanya, as to how there is no place for this whatsoever. In truth, this is a matter of the simple faith of **all** Bnei Yisrael, who are believers, the children of believers, that Hashem is the essence of good, that He actively leads the world, and that He oversees each and every person with individual providence. From this, the conclusion is understood that "everything the Merciful One does, He does for the good"—for the good of the person being overseen.

Since you write in your letter that you keep the *shiurim* of *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*, and that you review *maamarim* of *Chassidus*, surely for someone like you, an elaboration on the above is unnecessary. Contemplating this for even a short while will certainly suffice to improve your state of mind. Furthermore, in addition to all the matters involved in this, the saying of our Rebbeim is well known: "Think good and it will be good."

b) Regarding your brother, Mr. ... *shiya*, who has a *shidduch* opportunity and is in doubt as to whether he should disclose what he has gone through regarding his health, etc.:

As with all matters of a Jewish person, the solution to this question is also according to the *Shulchan Aruch*. Therefore, after he clarifies the details with the doctor who treated and is treating him, he should present his question before a *moreh hora'ah* (halachic authority) in Yisrael and act according to his instruction.

I was pleased to read in your letter about your brother’s participation in the activities of *Tzeirei Agudas Chabad*. Surely he will increase in this as well, in addition to the primary increase in *shiurim* in the *Torah* of *Chassidus Chabad* and following its guidance and customs.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above,

On behalf of the Rebbe, *shlita*,

Secretary

673

B"H, 23 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the Administration of the Chevra Gemilas Chasadim Shomrei Shabbos,

I was pleased to receive your letter of 22 Shevat, the report regarding the activities of the society in their holy work, gemilas chasadim, the fruits of which one eats in this world, while the principal remains for the World to Come.

Along with the news regarding the annual meeting on the evening of Shabbos Kodesh Mevarchim Chodesh Adar, which is coming upon us for good,

In which the 49th year—seven weeks—is concluded.

And according to the saying of the Baal Shem Tov, which we have heard many times from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that one must learn a lesson in the avodah of the Creator from everything, one can also say regarding this—in connection with this—that the year 5718 is the sixth year of the shmitah cycle according to the opinion of the Geonim. This is brought as a halachic ruling in the Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe (Hilchos Halva'ah, section 36). And the verse speaks specifically about this time: "For the seventh year, the year of shmitah, is approaching... you shall surely give to him" (and this is not enough, there is another condition), "and your heart shall not be grieved when you give to him" (and the reward for this is alongside it, as the conclusion and seal of the verse), "Hashem your G-d will bless you in all your works and in all your undertakings" (Devarim 15:9-10).

May it be the will of Hashem that even though the aforementioned verses speak of a time when we are not doing the will of the Omnipresent—as is written afterward, "For the destitute will not cease," etc., and as explained by our Sages in the Sifrei there—it is founded on the teaching of the Mishnah, "a mitzvah leads to another mitzvah." Especially the mitzvah of gemilas chasadim and tzedakah, which is equivalent to all the mitzvos. May this lead to a manner of avodah where we do the will of the Omnipresent, and may the prophecy be fulfilled: "There shall be no destitute among you."

With blessings for success in your holy work, with true expansion in both material and spiritual matters.

## 678

B.H., 26 Shevat, 5718
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

...On this occasion, I must address the conclusion of your letter, specifically the part where you write that they have not accomplished anything tangible, etc., etc.

How can a Yid judge a Yiddishe heart and state with such certainty that when one speaks words of hisorerus to another, it has surely not accomplished anything tangible? And furthermore, you immediately add that this is not only your personal opinion, but also the opinion of many others!

This is incorrect, even when one does not see anything tangible the next day or the day after. And in particular, I can inform you that one **has already** seen, and I hope one will continue to see, results. If only one would look at Yidden with the "right eye," it would increase the closeness of Yidden to one another, and that would increase the blessings of Hashem Yisbarach in everything that every individual needs, within the totality of Klal Yisrael. This is as indicated by the precise wording in the Shemoneh Esrei, "Bless us, our Father," (because) "all of us as one," and as is explained in many places, how great is the concept of shalom.

I want to hope that it is not as you write—that this is the sentiment of many—but rather that this is only a personal reaction, in accordance with the statement of Chazal, "Once one comes to a state of anger, etc."

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 681

[28 Shevat, 5718]

...It was a pleasure for me to read the beginning of the chapter regarding your holy activities—the dissemination of Yiddishkeit in general, and the dissemination of the wellsprings of Chassidus in particular.

Indeed, thank G-d for the impression that the visit made here upon those you write about. However, at the same time, it is understood that one must exercise extreme caution not to be satisfied with the aforementioned, and to say "my bowels are stirred" [i.e., to feel self-satisfied] simply because good results are already visible.

For—and I believe I have written about this several times—the impression made by a farbrengen or similar event is in the category of plowing, or at most, sowing. Many tasks—important tasks—must follow thereafter, so that there will be growth and the production of fruit as required.

On the contrary: since the plowing and sowing were successful—in which you invested effort and also saw *siyata d'shmaya*—it is understood that the responsibility of all those who must engage in the subsequent tasks required for the growth to proceed as it should, right up to the harvesting and gathering of the fruit, is many times greater.

Certainly, the above requires no further explanation, as the matter is quite simple. However, since the avodah in your location is in the stage of a beginning, this is the time to ensure that there is no overestimation—an exaggerated assessment—of phenomena, even of good and pleasant phenomena that should be appreciated, but not overestimated. And *kal v'chomer* [it is easy to understand].

It is understood that all of the above also applies to those who were impressed by the visit—they, too, require the aforementioned caution. That is to say: to know that the *hispaalus* is not the ultimate goal, and there must specifically be a *shov*—a return—to the observance of Torah and mitzvos, and ultimately to daily life.

May it be Hashem’s will that you bring good tidings in this regard as well, in an ever-increasing measure—as the Torah commands (and promises), "we ascend in matters of holiness."
